## Chapter: Introduction to Nonlinear Dynamics

### Introduction

The study of dynamics and vibration is a vast and complex field, with applications ranging from engineering to physics, and from mathematics to biology. In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of nonlinear dynamics, a subfield of dynamics that deals with systems that do not obey the superposition principle. 

Nonlinear dynamics is a field that has grown significantly over the past few decades, with the realization that many natural and man-made systems exhibit nonlinear behavior. This chapter will provide an introduction to the fundamental concepts and principles of nonlinear dynamics, setting the stage for a deeper exploration of this rich and diverse field.

The chapter begins by defining what we mean by a nonlinear system, and how it differs from a linear system. We will then explore some of the key characteristics of nonlinear systems, such as sensitivity to initial conditions, bifurcations, and chaos. 

We will also introduce some of the mathematical tools and techniques used to analyze nonlinear systems, including phase space diagrams, Lyapunov exponents, and Poincaré sections. These tools will be illustrated with examples from various fields, demonstrating the wide applicability of nonlinear dynamics.

Finally, we will discuss some of the practical applications of nonlinear dynamics, from the design of complex engineering systems to the modeling of biological processes. This will highlight the relevance and importance of nonlinear dynamics in our everyday lives.

In summary, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive introduction to nonlinear dynamics, equipping you with the knowledge and tools to further explore this exciting field. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional, we hope that this chapter will spark your interest and curiosity in nonlinear dynamics.

### Section: 7.1 Basic Concepts of Nonlinear Dynamics:

#### 7.1a Definition of Nonlinearity

In the previous chapter, we introduced the concept of linear systems, which obey the superposition principle. This principle states that the response of a system to a combination of inputs is the sum of the responses to each input individually. However, not all systems obey this principle. Those that do not are termed nonlinear systems.

A nonlinear system is one in which the output is not directly proportional to the input. In mathematical terms, a function $f(x)$ is nonlinear if it does not satisfy the properties of additivity and homogeneity. In other words, a nonlinear function does not obey the following conditions:

1. Additivity: $f(x + y) = f(x) + f(y)$
2. Homogeneity: $f(\alpha x) = \alpha f(x)$

where $x$ and $y$ are any inputs to the function, and $\alpha$ is any scalar.

The equation $f(x) = C$ is called linear if $f(x)$ is a linear map (as defined above) and nonlinear otherwise. The equation is called "homogeneous" if $C = 0$ and $f(x)$ is a homogeneous function.

In the context of dynamics and vibration, a nonlinear system is one in which the change in the system's state over time is not proportional to the change in the system's input. This can lead to complex and often unpredictable behavior, as small changes in the system's input can lead to large changes in the system's state. This sensitivity to initial conditions is a hallmark of nonlinear systems and is one of the reasons why they are of such interest to scientists and engineers.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the properties of nonlinear systems, exploring concepts such as bifurcations, chaos, and strange attractors. We will also introduce some of the mathematical tools used to analyze these systems, including phase space diagrams, Lyapunov exponents, and Poincaré sections.

#### 7.1b Types of Nonlinearities

Nonlinear systems can exhibit a wide range of behaviors, depending on the nature of their nonlinearity. In this section, we will introduce some of the most common types of nonlinearities encountered in dynamics and vibration.

1. **Softening and Hardening Nonlinearities**: These are often encountered in mechanical systems. A softening nonlinearity is one where the system's stiffness decreases as the amplitude of vibration increases. Conversely, a hardening nonlinearity is one where the system's stiffness increases with the amplitude of vibration. These types of nonlinearities can lead to phenomena such as jump phenomena and hysteresis.

2. **Cubic Nonlinearities**: These are nonlinearities that can be described by a cubic function. Cubic nonlinearities are common in many physical systems, including fluid dynamics and optics. They can lead to a variety of interesting behaviors, including period doubling and chaos.

3. **Saturation Nonlinearities**: These are nonlinearities where the system's response levels off or "saturates" beyond a certain input level. Saturation nonlinearities are common in electronic and control systems and can lead to limit cycles and other complex behaviors.

4. **Relay Nonlinearities**: These are nonlinearities where the system's response switches abruptly between two or more states. Relay nonlinearities are common in switching systems and can lead to oscillations and other complex behaviors.

5. **Frictional Nonlinearities**: These are nonlinearities caused by friction, which is inherently a nonlinear phenomenon. Frictional nonlinearities can lead to stick-slip behavior and other complex dynamics.

Each of these types of nonlinearities can lead to different types of behavior in a system, and understanding these behaviors is a key part of the study of nonlinear dynamics. In the following sections, we will explore some of these behaviors in more detail, and introduce some of the mathematical tools used to analyze them.

#### 7.1c Applications in Dynamics

Nonlinear dynamics has a wide range of applications in various fields, including engineering, physics, and biology. In this section, we will focus on its applications in dynamics, particularly in the context of factory automation infrastructure and the use of the Extended Kalman Filter (EKF).

##### Factory Automation Infrastructure

Factory automation infrastructure often involves complex, interconnected systems that exhibit nonlinear behavior. For instance, a kinematic chain, which is a series of mechanical components interconnected in a way that allows force transmission and motion, can exhibit nonlinear dynamics due to factors such as friction, backlash, and compliance. Understanding these nonlinearities is crucial for designing effective control strategies and ensuring the efficient operation of the automation system.

Nonlinear dynamics can also be used to model and analyze the behavior of implicit data structures used in factory automation. These data structures, which include various types of trees and graphs, can exhibit complex behaviors due to their inherent nonlinearities. By applying the concepts and tools of nonlinear dynamics, we can gain insights into these behaviors and develop more effective algorithms for data management and processing.

##### Extended Kalman Filter

The Extended Kalman Filter (EKF) is a powerful tool for state estimation in nonlinear systems. It extends the basic Kalman filter to handle nonlinear system dynamics and measurement models. The EKF operates in a predict-update cycle, using the system's dynamics to predict the state at the next time step, and then updating this prediction based on the actual measurement.

The EKF is particularly useful in situations where the system model is continuous-time, but measurements are taken at discrete time intervals. This is common in many physical systems, where state estimation is performed via a digital processor. The EKF handles this situation by discretizing the continuous-time system model, allowing for accurate state estimation even in the presence of nonlinearities.

The mathematical model of the EKF is given by:

$$
\dot{\mathbf{x}}(t) = f\bigl(\mathbf{x}(t), \mathbf{u}(t)\bigr) + \mathbf{w}(t) \quad \mathbf{w}(t) \sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{Q}(t)\bigr) \\
\mathbf{z}(t) = h\bigl(\mathbf{x}(t)\bigr) + \mathbf{v}(t) \quad \mathbf{v}(t) \sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{R}(t)\bigr)
$$

where $\mathbf{x}(t)$ is the state vector, $\mathbf{u}(t)$ is the control input, $\mathbf{w}(t)$ is the process noise, $\mathbf{z}(t)$ is the measurement, and $\mathbf{v}(t)$ is the measurement noise.

In the next sections, we will delve deeper into the mathematical foundations of nonlinear dynamics, and explore more applications in various fields.

### Section: 7.2 Nonlinear Oscillations:

#### 7.2a Introduction to Nonlinear Oscillations

Nonlinear oscillations are a fundamental aspect of nonlinear dynamics. They occur when the restoring force in a system is not proportional to the displacement, leading to a nonlinear differential equation that describes the system's motion. This nonlinearity can result in complex and interesting behaviors, such as bifurcations, chaos, and strange attractors, which are not observed in linear systems.

In this section, we will delve into the theory of nonlinear oscillations, exploring the mathematical techniques used to analyze them and the physical phenomena they can model. We will also discuss the Homotopy Analysis Method (HAM) and its application in frequency response analysis for nonlinear oscillators.

#### 7.2b Homotopy Analysis Method (HAM)

The Homotopy Analysis Method (HAM) is a powerful analytical tool for solving nonlinear differential equations. Unlike perturbation methods, which require small parameters, the HAM is applicable to general nonlinear problems without any restrictions.

The HAM has been reported to be useful for obtaining analytical solutions for nonlinear frequency response equations. These solutions can capture various nonlinear behaviors such as hardening-type, softening-type, or mixed behaviors of the oscillator. These analytical equations are also useful in predicting chaos in nonlinear systems.

Consider a general solution to the zeroth-order problem:

$$
Y_0(t,t_1) = A(t_1)\, e^{+it} + A^\ast(t_1)\, e^{-it},
$$

where $A(t_1)$ is a complex-valued amplitude to the zeroth-order solution $Y_0(t, t_1)$ and $i^2 = -1$. The forcing in the right-hand side of the differential equation is given by:

$$
\left[ -3\, A^2\, A^\ast - 2\, i\, \frac{dA}{dt_1} \right]\, e^{+it} - A^3\, e^{+3it} + c.c.
$$

where "c.c." denotes the complex conjugate of the preceding terms. The occurrence of "secular terms" can be prevented by imposing on the – yet unknown – amplitude $A(t_1)$ the "solvability condition":

$$
-3\, A^2\, A^\ast - 2\, i\, \frac{dA}{dt_1} = 0.
$$

The solution to the solvability condition, also satisfying the initial conditions, is:

$$
A = \tfrac 1 2\, \exp \left(\tfrac 3 8\, i \, t_1 \right).
$$

As a result, the approximate solution by the multiple-scales analysis is:

$$
y(t) = \cos \left[ \left( 1 + \tfrac38\, \varepsilon \right) t \right] + \mathcal{O}(\varepsilon),
$$

This solution is valid until $t = O(\epsilon^{-2})$. Higher-order solutions – using the method of multiple scales – require the introduction of additional slow scales, i.e., $t_2, t_3$, etc. However, this introduces additional complexity into the analysis.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the application of HAM in nonlinear oscillations, exploring its use in various physical systems and its implications for the study of nonlinear dynamics.

#### 7.2b Nonlinear Oscillator Equations

Nonlinear oscillator equations are a fundamental part of the study of nonlinear dynamics. These equations describe the motion of a system where the restoring force is not proportional to the displacement. This nonlinearity can lead to complex behaviors such as bifurcations, chaos, and strange attractors, which are not observed in linear systems.

One of the most common examples of a nonlinear oscillator is the Duffing oscillator, described by the equation:

$$
\frac{d^2x}{dt^2} + \delta \frac{dx}{dt} + \alpha x + \beta x^3 = \gamma \cos(\omega t),
$$

where $\alpha$, $\beta$, $\delta$, $\gamma$, and $\omega$ are constants, and $x$ is the displacement. This equation describes a variety of physical systems, including mechanical systems with nonlinear springs, electrical circuits with nonlinear elements, and even certain types of weather systems.

The Duffing oscillator exhibits a rich variety of behaviors, depending on the values of the parameters. For certain parameter values, the system exhibits periodic behavior, while for others it can exhibit chaotic behavior.

Another important class of nonlinear oscillators is described by the Van der Pol equation:

$$
\frac{d^2x}{dt^2} - \mu(1 - x^2)\frac{dx}{dt} + x = 0,
$$

where $\mu$ is a parameter and $x$ is the displacement. This equation was originally derived to model the behavior of certain types of vacuum tubes, but it has since been found to describe a wide variety of physical systems, including certain types of biological oscillators.

The Van der Pol oscillator is interesting because it exhibits a behavior known as relaxation oscillations, where the system alternates between periods of rapid change and periods of slow change. This behavior is characteristic of many physical systems, including certain types of biological oscillators and electronic circuits.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the analysis of these and other types of nonlinear oscillators, exploring the mathematical techniques used to analyze them and the physical phenomena they can model.

#### 7.2c Applications in Dynamics

Nonlinear oscillations have a wide range of applications in various fields of dynamics. In this section, we will explore some of these applications, focusing on factory automation infrastructure and the use of the Extended Kalman filter.

##### Factory Automation Infrastructure

In factory automation infrastructure, nonlinear oscillations can be observed in the kinematic chain of robotic arms. The kinematic chain, which is a series of links connected by joints, is responsible for the movement and positioning of the robotic arm. The dynamics of this system can be described by a set of nonlinear differential equations, which take into account the geometry of the links and the forces and torques at the joints.

For example, consider a simple two-link robotic arm. The equations of motion for this system can be written as:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\tau_1 &= I_1 \ddot{\theta}_1 + I_2 \ddot{\theta}_2 + d_2 m_2 \cos(\theta_2) \ddot{\theta}_1 + d_2 m_2 \sin(\theta_2) \dot{\theta}_1^2 \\
\tau_2 &= I_2 \ddot{\theta}_2 - d_2 m_2 \cos(\theta_2) \ddot{\theta}_1,
\end{aligned}
$$

where $\tau_1$ and $\tau_2$ are the torques at the joints, $I_1$ and $I_2$ are the moments of inertia of the links, $m_2$ is the mass of the second link, $d_2$ is the distance from the joint to the center of mass of the second link, and $\theta_1$ and $\theta_2$ are the angles of the links. These equations are nonlinear due to the presence of the sine and cosine terms, which describe the coupling between the two links.

##### Extended Kalman Filter

The Extended Kalman filter (EKF) is a powerful tool for state estimation in nonlinear systems. It extends the basic Kalman filter to handle systems described by nonlinear equations. The EKF operates in a predict-update cycle, using the system's dynamics and measurements to estimate the state of the system.

The EKF can be applied to a wide range of systems, including those described by the Duffing and Van der Pol equations discussed in the previous section. For example, in a system described by the Duffing equation, the EKF can be used to estimate the displacement $x$ based on measurements of the system's output and knowledge of the system's parameters.

The EKF uses a set of differential equations to predict the state of the system and update the state estimate based on the measurements. These equations are given by:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\dot{\hat{\mathbf{x}}}(t) &= f\bigl(\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t),\mathbf{u}(t)\bigr)+\mathbf{K}(t)\Bigl(\mathbf{z}(t)-h\bigl(\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t)\bigr)\Bigr)\\
\dot{\mathbf{P}}(t) &= \mathbf{F}(t)\mathbf{P}(t)+\mathbf{P}(t)\mathbf{F}(t)^{T}-\mathbf{K}(t)\mathbf{H}(t)\mathbf{P}(t)+\mathbf{Q}(t)\\
\mathbf{K}(t) &= \mathbf{P}(t)\mathbf{H}(t)^{T}\mathbf{R}(t)^{-1}\\
\mathbf{F}(t) &= \left . \frac{\partial f}{\partial \mathbf{x} } \right \vert _{\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t),\mathbf{u}(t)}\\
\mathbf{H}(t) &= \left . \frac{\partial h}{\partial \mathbf{x} } \right \vert _{\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t)} 
\end{aligned}
$$

where $\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t)$ is the state estimate, $\mathbf{u}(t)$ is the input, $\mathbf{z}(t)$ is the measurement, $\mathbf{P}(t)$ is the state covariance, $\mathbf{K}(t)$ is the Kalman gain, $\mathbf{F}(t)$ is the Jacobian of the system dynamics with respect to the state, and $\mathbf{H}(t)$ is the Jacobian of the measurement model with respect to the state.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the analysis of nonlinear oscillations and their applications in various fields of dynamics.

### Section: 7.3 Bifurcations and Chaos:

#### 7.3a Introduction to Bifurcations

Bifurcation theory is a field within mathematics that studies and classifies phenomena characterized by sudden shifts in behavior arising from small changes in circumstances, affecting diverse and interconnected systems. One of the most common types of bifurcations in dynamical systems is the pitchfork bifurcation.

##### Pitchfork Bifurcation

In a pitchfork bifurcation, the system transitions from one fixed point to three fixed points. There are two types of pitchfork bifurcations: supercritical and subcritical. These bifurcations occur generically in continuous dynamical systems described by ordinary differential equations (ODEs) that exhibit symmetry.

###### Supercritical Case

The normal form of the supercritical pitchfork bifurcation is given by the equation:

$$
\dot{x} = rx - x^3
$$

In this case, for $r<0$, there is one stable equilibrium at $x = 0$. However, for $r>0$, there is an unstable equilibrium at $x = 0$, and two stable equilibria at $x = \pm\sqrt{r}$.

###### Subcritical Case

The normal form for the subcritical case is given by the equation:

$$
\dot{x} = rx + x^3
$$

In this case, for $r<0$, the equilibrium at $x=0$ is stable, and there are two unstable equilibria at $x = \pm \sqrt{-r}$. For $r>0$, the equilibrium at $x=0$ is unstable.

###### Formal Definition

An ODE described by a one parameter function $f(x, r)$ with $ r \in \mathbb{R}$ has a pitchfork bifurcation at $(x, r) = (0, r_0)$. The form of the pitchfork is given by the sign of the third derivative. Note that subcritical and supercritical describe the stability of the outer lines of the pitchfork (dashed or solid, respectively) and are not dependent on which direction the pitchfork faces.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the implications of these bifurcations and their role in the onset of chaos in dynamical systems.

#### 7.3b Types of Bifurcations

In the study of dynamical systems, bifurcations are classified into different types based on their characteristics and the nature of the changes they induce in the system's behavior. In the previous section, we discussed the pitchfork bifurcation, a local bifurcation that results in the system transitioning from one fixed point to three fixed points. In this section, we will explore other types of bifurcations, their properties, and their implications for the dynamics of the system.

##### Saddle-Node Bifurcation

A saddle-node bifurcation, also known as a fold bifurcation, is a local bifurcation in which a pair of fixed points, one stable and one unstable, collide and annihilate each other. This bifurcation is characterized by the normal form:

$$
\dot{x} = r + x^2
$$

where $r$ is the bifurcation parameter. For $r<0$, there are two fixed points, one stable and one unstable. For $r=0$, the two fixed points collide and annihilate each other, and for $r>0$, there are no fixed points.

##### Transcritical Bifurcation

A transcritical bifurcation is another local bifurcation in which two fixed points exchange stability. The normal form of a transcritical bifurcation is:

$$
\dot{x} = rx - x^2
$$

where $r$ is the bifurcation parameter. For $r<0$, there are two fixed points, one at $x=0$ which is stable, and one at $x=r$ which is unstable. For $r=0$, both fixed points coincide at $x=0$ and are semi-stable. For $r>0$, the fixed point at $x=0$ becomes unstable, and the fixed point at $x=r$ becomes stable.

##### Hopf Bifurcation

A Hopf bifurcation is a local bifurcation in which a stable fixed point loses stability as a pair of complex conjugate eigenvalues cross the imaginary axis of the complex plane. This bifurcation leads to the birth of a limit cycle and is characterized by the normal form:

$$
\dot{x} = r x - y - x (x^2 + y^2)
$$
$$
\dot{y} = x + r y - y (x^2 + y^2)
$$

where $r$ is the bifurcation parameter. For $r<0$, there is a stable fixed point at the origin. For $r=0$, the fixed point at the origin becomes semi-stable, and for $r>0$, the fixed point loses stability and a stable limit cycle is born.

Understanding these bifurcations and their properties is crucial for the study of nonlinear dynamics and chaos. In the next section, we will discuss the concept of chaos and its role in dynamical systems.

#### 7.3c Introduction to Chaos

Chaos theory is a branch of mathematics that deals with nonlinear dynamical systems, systems whose behavior can in principle be determined exactly from their initial conditions but which in practice exhibit apparently random and unpredictable behavior due to their extreme sensitivity to initial conditions. This sensitivity is often referred to as the butterfly effect, a term coined by Edward Lorenz, one of the pioneers of chaos theory.

##### The Lorenz System

The Lorenz system is a system of ordinary differential equations first studied by Edward Lorenz. It is notable for having chaotic solutions for certain parameter values and initial conditions. The system is defined by:

$$
\begin{align*}
\dot{x} &= \sigma(y - x) \\
\dot{y} &= x(\rho - z) - y \\
\dot{z} &= xy - \beta z
\end{align*}
$$

where $\sigma$, $\rho$, and $\beta$ are system parameters. The Lorenz system is a simplified mathematical model for atmospheric convection and is one of the most studied examples of dynamical systems that exhibit chaotic behavior.

##### Smale's 14th Problem and the Existence of Strange Attractors

In 1967, mathematician Stephen Smale posed a list of 18 unsolved problems in mathematics, one of which (the 14th) asked whether the properties of the Lorenz attractor exhibit that of a strange attractor. A strange attractor is a type of attractor in dynamical systems that exhibits sensitive dependence on initial conditions and has a fractal structure.

The problem was answered affirmatively by Warwick Tucker in 2002. Tucker used rigorous numerical methods, including interval arithmetic and normal forms, to prove the existence of a strange attractor in the Lorenz system. This marked a significant milestone in the study of chaotic dynamical systems and further established the Lorenz system as a fundamental example of a system exhibiting chaotic behavior.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of strange attractors, their properties, and their role in chaotic dynamical systems.

#### 7.4a Nonlinear Dynamics in Mechanical Systems

Nonlinear dynamics plays a crucial role in the field of mechanical engineering. The behavior of many mechanical systems can be described using nonlinear differential equations, which often lead to complex and interesting dynamics. In this section, we will explore some of the key concepts and applications of nonlinear dynamics in mechanical systems.

##### Nonlinear Oscillations

One of the most common applications of nonlinear dynamics in mechanical systems is in the study of oscillations. In many real-world systems, the restoring force is not proportional to the displacement, leading to nonlinear oscillations. The simple harmonic oscillator, a linear system, is often used as a first approximation, but it fails to capture the behavior of many systems.

A common example of a nonlinear oscillator is the pendulum. For small angles, the pendulum can be approximated as a simple harmonic oscillator, but for larger angles, this approximation fails. The equation of motion for a pendulum of length $l$ and mass $m$ under the influence of gravity $g$ is given by:

$$
\frac{d^2\theta}{dt^2} + \frac{g}{l} \sin(\theta) = 0
$$

where $\theta$ is the angle the pendulum makes with the vertical. This is a nonlinear differential equation due to the $\sin(\theta)$ term.

##### Nonlinear Resonance

Resonance is a phenomenon that occurs when a system is driven at its natural frequency, leading to large amplitude oscillations. In linear systems, the resonance behavior is relatively simple. However, in nonlinear systems, the resonance behavior can be much more complex due to the dependence of the natural frequency on the amplitude of oscillation.

This can lead to phenomena such as hardening or softening behavior, where the natural frequency of the system increases or decreases with increasing amplitude, respectively. This has important implications in many engineering applications, such as the design of bridges and buildings to withstand earthquakes.

##### Bifurcations and Chaos

Nonlinear systems can also exhibit bifurcations, where a small change in a system parameter leads to a qualitative change in the system's behavior. This can lead to the onset of chaos, where the system exhibits sensitive dependence on initial conditions and long-term prediction becomes impossible.

In the next section, we will explore some specific examples of nonlinear dynamics in mechanical engineering applications, including the analysis of vibrations in structures, the dynamics of rotating machinery, and the control of robotic systems.

#### 7.4b Nonlinear Dynamics in Electrical Systems

Nonlinear dynamics also plays a significant role in electrical systems. One of the most studied examples of nonlinear dynamics in electrical systems is Chua's circuit. This circuit, named after Leon Chua, who invented it in 1983, is a simple electronic circuit that exhibits complex nonlinear dynamics, including chaos.

##### Chua's Circuit

Chua's circuit consists of two capacitors, an inductor, a linear resistor, and a nonlinear resistor (Chua's diode). The dynamics of Chua's circuit can be modeled by a system of three nonlinear ordinary differential equations in the variables $x(t)$, $y(t)$, and $z(t)$, which represent the voltages across the capacitors C1 and C2 and the electric current in the inductor L1 respectively. These equations are:

$$
\begin{align*}
\frac{dx}{dt} &= α(y - x - f(x)) \\
\frac{dy}{dt} &= x - y + z \\
\frac{dz}{dt} &= -βy
\end{align*}
$$

The function $f(x)$ describes the electrical response of the nonlinear resistor, and its shape depends on the particular configuration of its components. The parameters α and β are determined by the particular values of the circuit components.

##### Chaotic Behavior in Chua's Circuit

A computer-assisted proof of chaotic behavior in Chua's circuit was published in 1997. A self-excited chaotic attractor, known as "the double scroll" because of its shape in the ($x$, $y$, $z$) space, was first observed in a circuit containing a nonlinear element such that $f(x)$ was a 3-segment piecewise-linear function.

The easy experimental implementation of the circuit, combined with the existence of a simple and accurate theoretical model, makes Chua's circuit a useful system to study many fundamental and applied issues of chaos theory. Because of this, it has been the object of much study and appears widely referenced in the literature.

##### Chua's Circuit with a Memristor

The Chua diode can also be replaced by a memristor, a two-terminal electronic device whose resistance depends on the amount and direction of charge that has previously flowed through the device. An experimental setup that implemented Chua's chaotic circuit with a memristor was demonstrated by Muthuswamy in 2009; the memristor was actually implemented with active components in this experiment.

The use of a memristor in Chua's circuit introduces additional nonlinear dynamics, making the system even more complex and interesting to study. This has important implications in many areas of electrical engineering and computer science, including the design of neural networks and other complex systems.

#### 7.4c Nonlinear Dynamics in Control Systems

Nonlinear dynamics is a critical aspect of control systems, particularly in the design and analysis of controllers for nonlinear systems. The application of nonlinear dynamics in control systems can be seen in the use of the Higher-order Sinusoidal Input Describing Function (HOSIDF) and the Extended Kalman Filter (EKF).

##### Higher-order Sinusoidal Input Describing Function in Control Systems

The HOSIDF is a powerful tool in the analysis and design of control systems. It provides a natural extension of the widely used sinusoidal describing functions when nonlinearities cannot be neglected. The HOSIDF is intuitive in its identification and interpretation, providing direct information about the behavior of the system in practice. 

In the context of control systems, the HOSIDF has two distinct applications. First, due to their ease of identification, HOSIDFs provide a tool for on-site testing during system design. This allows for the identification and correction of potential issues early in the design process, reducing the likelihood of costly redesigns later on. 

Second, the application of HOSIDFs to nonlinear controller design for nonlinear systems has been shown to yield significant advantages over conventional time domain-based tuning. This is because the HOSIDF provides a more accurate representation of the system's behavior, allowing for the design of more effective controllers.

##### Extended Kalman Filter in Control Systems

The Extended Kalman Filter (EKF) is another tool used in the analysis and design of control systems. The EKF is a generalization of the Kalman filter that can handle nonlinear systems. The EKF operates by linearizing the system about the current estimate, and then applying the standard Kalman filter to this linearized system.

The EKF is particularly useful in control systems where the system dynamics are nonlinear and the system is subject to Gaussian noise. The EKF provides a method for estimating the system's state, which can then be used in the design of the controller. 

The EKF is defined by the following equations:

$$
\begin{align*}
\dot{\mathbf{x}}(t) &= f\bigl(\mathbf{x}(t), \mathbf{u}(t)\bigr) + \mathbf{w}(t) &\mathbf{w}(t) &\sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{Q}(t)\bigr) \\
\mathbf{z}(t) &= h\bigl(\mathbf{x}(t)\bigr) + \mathbf{v}(t) &\mathbf{v}(t) &\sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{R}(t)\bigr)
\end{align*}
$$

where $\mathbf{x}(t)$ is the state vector, $\mathbf{u}(t)$ is the control input, $\mathbf{w}(t)$ is the process noise, $\mathbf{z}(t)$ is the measurement, and $\mathbf{v}(t)$ is the measurement noise. The functions $f$ and $h$ represent the system dynamics and measurement model, respectively.

In conclusion, nonlinear dynamics plays a crucial role in the design and analysis of control systems. The use of tools such as the HOSIDF and the EKF allows for the effective design of controllers for nonlinear systems, leading to improved system performance.

### Section: 7.5 Recitation:

#### 7.5a Review of Key Concepts

In this section, we will review the key concepts that have been discussed in this chapter. The focus of this chapter has been on introducing the principles and applications of nonlinear dynamics, particularly in the context of control systems.

##### Nonlinear Dynamics in Control Systems

Nonlinear dynamics is a critical aspect of control systems, particularly in the design and analysis of controllers for nonlinear systems. The application of nonlinear dynamics in control systems can be seen in the use of the Higher-order Sinusoidal Input Describing Function (HOSIDF) and the Extended Kalman Filter (EKF).

##### Higher-order Sinusoidal Input Describing Function (HOSIDF)

The HOSIDF is a powerful tool in the analysis and design of control systems. It provides a natural extension of the widely used sinusoidal describing functions when nonlinearities cannot be neglected. The HOSIDF is intuitive in its identification and interpretation, providing direct information about the behavior of the system in practice. 

In the context of control systems, the HOSIDF has two distinct applications. First, due to their ease of identification, HOSIDFs provide a tool for on-site testing during system design. This allows for the identification and correction of potential issues early in the design process, reducing the likelihood of costly redesigns later on. 

Second, the application of HOSIDFs to nonlinear controller design for nonlinear systems has been shown to yield significant advantages over conventional time domain-based tuning. This is because the HOSIDF provides a more accurate representation of the system's behavior, allowing for the design of more effective controllers.

##### Extended Kalman Filter (EKF)

The Extended Kalman Filter (EKF) is another tool used in the analysis and design of control systems. The EKF is a generalization of the Kalman filter that can handle nonlinear systems. The EKF operates by linearizing the system about the current estimate, and then applying the standard Kalman filter to this linearized system.

The EKF is particularly useful in control systems where the system dynamics are nonlinear and the system is subject to Gaussian noise. The EKF provides a method for estimating the state of a system in real-time, even in the presence of noise, making it a valuable tool in the design and analysis of control systems.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical foundations of these concepts, providing a more rigorous understanding of their applications in control systems.

#### 7.5b Problem Solving Session

In this section, we will apply the concepts of nonlinear dynamics, specifically the Higher-order Sinusoidal Input Describing Function (HOSIDF) and the Extended Kalman Filter (EKF), to solve problems related to control systems.

##### Problem 1: Application of HOSIDF

Consider a nonlinear system with a sinusoidal input. The system's response is not a simple sinusoid due to the presence of nonlinearities. Your task is to use the HOSIDF to analyze the system's behavior.

**Solution:**

To solve this problem, we first need to identify the system's nonlinearities and represent them using the HOSIDF. This involves expressing the system's response as a sum of sinusoids with different frequencies and amplitudes, which are determined by the system's nonlinearities.

Next, we use the identified HOSIDF to design a controller for the system. This involves using the HOSIDF to predict the system's response to different control inputs and selecting the input that results in the desired system behavior.

##### Problem 2: Application of EKF

Consider a nonlinear system with a noisy measurement. Your task is to use the EKF to estimate the system's state.

**Solution:**

To solve this problem, we first need to linearize the system's dynamics and measurement equations around the current state estimate. This is done using the Jacobian matrices of the system's dynamics and measurement equations.

Next, we use the linearized system to compute the Kalman gain, which determines how much the state estimate is updated based on the measurement.

Finally, we use the Kalman gain to update the state estimate and the state estimate covariance. This involves a prediction step, where the state estimate and the state estimate covariance are propagated forward in time, and an update step, where the state estimate and the state estimate covariance are updated based on the measurement.

These problems illustrate the practical applications of the concepts discussed in this chapter. By understanding and applying these concepts, you will be able to analyze and design control systems for nonlinear systems.

#### 7.5c Discussion and Q&A

In this section, we will discuss the concepts of nonlinear dynamics, specifically the Higher-order Sinusoidal Input Describing Function (HOSIDF) and the Extended Kalman Filter (EKF), and answer some common questions related to these topics.

##### Q1: Why do we need to use the HOSIDF for analyzing nonlinear systems?

**Answer:**

Nonlinear systems are inherently complex and their behavior can be difficult to predict. The HOSIDF provides a way to represent the system's response to sinusoidal inputs, which can be easier to analyze than the system's response to arbitrary inputs. By expressing the system's response as a sum of sinusoids with different frequencies and amplitudes, we can gain insights into the system's behavior and design controllers to achieve desired system performance.

##### Q2: How does the EKF handle the nonlinearity in the system's dynamics and measurement equations?

**Answer:**

The EKF handles the nonlinearity by linearizing the system's dynamics and measurement equations around the current state estimate. This is done using the Jacobian matrices of the system's dynamics and measurement equations. The linearized system is then used to compute the Kalman gain and update the state estimate and the state estimate covariance. This approach allows the EKF to provide accurate state estimates even for nonlinear systems.

##### Q3: What are the limitations of the HOSIDF and the EKF?

**Answer:**

The HOSIDF is based on the assumption that the system's response to a sinusoidal input can be represented as a sum of sinusoids. If this assumption is not valid, the HOSIDF may not provide accurate results. 

The EKF, on the other hand, relies on the assumption that the system's dynamics and measurement equations can be accurately linearized around the current state estimate. If this assumption is not valid, the EKF may not provide accurate state estimates. Furthermore, the EKF assumes that the system's state and measurement noise are Gaussian, which may not be the case for all systems.

These limitations highlight the importance of understanding the underlying assumptions and limitations of the tools we use to analyze and control nonlinear systems.

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of nonlinear dynamics, a field that has significant implications for the study of dynamics and vibration. We have explored the fundamental concepts and principles that underpin nonlinear dynamics, and we have seen how these principles can be applied to a variety of practical scenarios.

We have learned that nonlinear dynamics is a field that deals with systems in which the output is not directly proportional to the input. This nonlinearity can lead to complex and unpredictable behavior, including chaos, bifurcations, and strange attractors. We have also seen how nonlinear dynamics can be used to model and analyze a wide range of physical phenomena, from the oscillations of a pendulum to the vibrations of a bridge.

In addition, we have discussed the mathematical tools and techniques that are used to study nonlinear dynamics, including phase space diagrams, Lyapunov exponents, and Poincaré sections. These tools allow us to visualize and quantify the behavior of nonlinear systems, and to predict their future behavior under different conditions.

In conclusion, the study of nonlinear dynamics provides us with a powerful framework for understanding the complex and often unpredictable behavior of dynamical systems. By applying the principles and techniques of nonlinear dynamics, we can gain new insights into the nature of dynamics and vibration, and develop more effective strategies for controlling and manipulating these phenomena.
